<div id="review_97251320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97251320" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1512868-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269709619p2/1512868.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97251320?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/1512868-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">My mum</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=been-around-the-world-and-i-i-i">been-around-the-world-and-i-i-i</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97251320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15202896640451528343">Haunting book about the disintegration of a Native American community in North Dakota in the early 20th century, as the land they live on is sold off to white developers.<br/><br/>Told in alternating chapters by two narrators - Nanapush, an old man of the tribe, still living as much by the old ways as he can, and Pauline, a youth at the beginning who unravels as she discards her heritage and comes under the influence of Christianity - revolving around their connections to Fleur Pillager, a fierce and inde</span>
  <span id="freeText15202896640451528343" style="display:none">Haunting book about the disintegration of a Native American community in North Dakota in the early 20th century, as the land they live on is sold off to white developers.<br /><br />Told in alternating chapters by two narrators - Nanapush, an old man of the tribe, still living as much by the old ways as he can, and Pauline, a youth at the beginning who unravels as she discards her heritage and comes under the influence of Christianity - revolving around their connections to Fleur Pillager, a fierce and independent woman whom is largely feared by the community. Through this we get to see not only the effects of the loss of land (and resulting loss of community) but also the havoc wreaked in Fleur's life, mostly set in motion by Pauline.<br /><br />I detested Pauline by the end, as mad or not she was incredibly vindictive, but loved the voice of Nanapush and couldn't help but feel his loss as those he loved around him fell away and the old ways slipped. Incredibly poetic, I'll definitely look out for more from Erdrich.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15202896640451528343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15064884554475997027">This is the third book I’ve read by Louise Erdrich and I think it might be my favorite so far. I loved the magical realism elements and the poetic language. The story she tells is important, and the characters are interesting. The only problems I had it with it were my own: I tend to read at a pretty fast clip, and there are parts of this book that definitely want to be savored and read more slowly than I’m used to. It’s not that the writing is difficult; it’s more like it wants to immerse you i</span>
  <span id="freeText15064884554475997027" style="display:none">This is the third book I’ve read by Louise Erdrich and I think it might be my favorite so far. I loved the magical realism elements and the poetic language. The story she tells is important, and the characters are interesting. The only problems I had it with it were my own: I tend to read at a pretty fast clip, and there are parts of this book that definitely want to be savored and read more slowly than I’m used to. It’s not that the writing is difficult; it’s more like it wants to immerse you in it, and that can take some moments. This book, like any good novel should, gave me a lot to think about and is worthy of a rereading. And like usual when I finish one of those types of books, I wish I had someone to talk to about it!</span>

<div id="review_1178715554" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1178715554" />
    <a title="Krista" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4729639-krista"><img alt="Krista" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579967825p2/4729639.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1178715554?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista" href="/user/show/4729639-krista">Krista</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4729639-krista?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4729639-krista?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4729639-krista?shelf=indigenous">indigenous</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1178715554" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11807321961546882526"><i>
  <blockquote>We started dying before the snow, and like the snow, we continued to fall.</blockquote>
</i><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78887.Tracks' title='Tracks by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tracks</a> is exactly my favourite kind of book: a fresh viewpoint on history that blends fact with poetic language, imprinting both my mind and heart with an author's voice and vision. In this case, the time is early 20th century, the place is the Anishinabe Reservation in North Dakota, and author Louise Erdrich uses a blend of magical realism, Catholic mysticism, and Native mythology to reinterpret the accepted narrative</span>
  <span id="freeText11807321961546882526" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>We started dying before the snow, and like the snow, we continued to fall.</blockquote>
</i><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78887.Tracks" title="Tracks by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">Tracks</a> is exactly my favourite kind of book: a fresh viewpoint on history that blends fact with poetic language, imprinting both my mind and heart with an author's voice and vision. In this case, the time is early 20th century, the place is the Anishinabe Reservation in North Dakota, and author Louise Erdrich uses a blend of magical realism, Catholic mysticism, and Native mythology to reinterpret the accepted narrative of western expansionism.<br /><br />As <i>Tracks</i> opens, the year is 1912, and after surviving the genocide that forced the Anishinabe onto a reservation that represented a fraction of their former traditional lands, those who also survived the "pox and fever" were now succumbing to consumption in unbelievable numbers.<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>By then, we thought disaster must surely have spent its force, that disease must have claimed all of the Anishinabe that the earth could hold and bury. But the earth is limitless and so is luck and so were our people once.</blockquote>
</i><br />This speaker is Nanapush, an Anishinabe who, at 50 in 1912, is considered an elder of the decimated tribe. He is speaking to his granddaughter many years after the events of the book; attempting to explain the actions of her mother (Fleur Pillager) that led to their alienation. The chapters alternate between his point-of-view and that of Pauline Puyat; an orphan of mixed blood who has her own opinion of Fleur and who makes very different choices about how to survive in their changing world. Fleur, the last living member of her own family, could be considered the main character of <i>Tracks</i>, and it's interesting that her actions are only described in the third-person; interpreted by two such different characters. As consumption gives way to famine and influenza -- and as pressure is put on the remaining Anishinabe to sell off their land to logging companies -- this era is truly post-apocalyptic to the tribe; a people who must decide if survival means clinging to traditional ways or assimilating into the culture of their overlords. (And "post-apocalyptic" isn't hyperbole: if an alien invading force did to all of humanity what white settlers did to the Anishinabe tribe, the term "post-apocalyptic" would probably be considered a mild term.)<br /><br />That is, broadly, the plot but it's the <i>writing</i> that had me hooked throughout this book. The book is set on the shores of Lake Matchimanito and here is a description of the water monster who lives in its depths; a creature that all Anishinabe mothers warn their daughters against:<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>(Misshepeshu) appears with green eyes, copper skin, a mouth tender as a child's. But if you fell into his arms, he sprouts horns, fangs, claws, fins. His feet are joined as one and his skin, brass scales, rings to the touch. You're fascinated, cannot move. He casts a shell necklace at your feet, weeps gleaming chips that harden into mica on your breasts. He holds you under. Then he takes the body of a lion, a fat brown worm, or a familiar man. He's made of gold. He's made of beach moss. He's a thing of dry foam, a thing of death by drowning, the death a Chippewa cannot survive.</blockquote>
</i><br />Fleur Pillager, who takes up solitary residence on a remote shoreline of this lake, is a fascination to her tribe because she seems to have tamed Misshepeshu and assumed some of his powers:<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>She messed with evil, laughed at the old women's advice and dressed like a man. She got herself into some half-forgotten medicine, studied ways we shouldn't talk about. Some say she kept the finger of a child in her pocket and a powder of unborn rabbits in a leather thong around her neck. She laid the heart of an owl on her tongue so she could see at night, and went out, hunting, not even in her own body. We know for sure because the next morning, in the snow or dust, we followed the tracks of her bare feet and saw where they changed, where the claws sprang out, the pad broadened and pressed into the dirt.</blockquote>
</i><br />In contrast, Pauline enters the white man's world, and as an end-of-life caregiver, has her first ecstatic experience when she witnesses her first death:<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>I stood when she was gone and called the others into the room, surprised at how light I felt, as though I'd been cut free as well. I hooked my hands on a chair, just to hold steady. If I took off my shoes I would rise into the air. If I took my hands away from my face I would smile. A cool blackness lifted me, out of the room and through the door. I leapt, spun, landing along the edge of the clearing. My body rippled. I tore leaves off a branch and stuffed them into my mouth to smother laughter. The wind shook in the trees. The sky hardened to light. And that is when, twirling dizzily, my wings raked the air, and I rose in three powerful beats and saw what lay below…I alone, watching, filled with breath, knew death as a form of grace.</blockquote>
</i><br />I'm not usually a fan of magical realism -- where there are no natural laws, there's very little at stake -- but using Native mythology and Catholic mysticism (saints were always levitating and Pauline surrenders to the church more and more as time goes by) as both contrasting belief systems and as <i>established</i> belief systems (millions of believers agree to the facts of each system: that people <i>can</i> fly when in communion with their gods) removes the actions from the realm of magic and makes them perfectly logical (in the context of the story). While <i>Tracks</i> could have had a bitter or hectoring tone, it's playful from Nanapush's point-of-view, and Pauline's story is one of a descent into madness -- it's a story of survival that gives equal value to the different choices that the people felt they had to make.<br /><br /><i>Tracks</i> was interesting to me on so many different levels and, as it's apparently a part of a cycle of books by Erdrich, I'll be revisiting these characters as soon as I can.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11807321961546882526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3119934568" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3119934568" />
    <a title="gwayle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2514493-gwayle"><img alt="gwayle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263184933p2/2514493.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3119934568?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="gwayle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="gwayle" href="/user/show/2514493-gwayle">gwayle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2514493-gwayle?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3119934568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13591275699648685491">This beautifully crafted novel follows two women—orphans whose paths diverge and intersect in intense ways—on an Anishinaabe/Ojibwe reservation in North Dakota through the 1910s and into the 1920s. In alternating chapters, Pauline Puyat tells her own story of leaving her family (by choice) at a young age and ending up a passionate Christian convert; Fleur Pillager's story—told through a father figure, Nanapush, and addressed to Fleur's estranged daughter—is one of high stakes and desperate deeds</span>
  <span id="freeText13591275699648685491" style="display:none">This beautifully crafted novel follows two women—orphans whose paths diverge and intersect in intense ways—on an Anishinaabe/Ojibwe reservation in North Dakota through the 1910s and into the 1920s. In alternating chapters, Pauline Puyat tells her own story of leaving her family (by choice) at a young age and ending up a passionate Christian convert; Fleur Pillager's story—told through a father figure, Nanapush, and addressed to Fleur's estranged daughter—is one of high stakes and desperate deeds as she tries to hang on to her ancestral land. The backdrop is a tribe reduced by waves of sickness and starvation, now in the crosshairs of government agents who want to sell the land to lumber interests. Erdrich does a lot in few pages to set a larger scene and introduce many wonderful characters (Margaret Kashpaw—wow); this novel is the earliest of a cycle of novels. It's always a joy to read novels of such richness and depth, such craft and scope. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13591275699648685491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3052204881" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7780123189410689380">I am on an enjoyable journey to read all of Louise Erdrich's novels. Tracks is my third novel of hers and the first in the Love Medicine read-along in which many from bookstagram are participating (thanks Erin and Dani for organizing!). <br/><br/>This book exemplifies Erdrich's writing: PERFECT paragraphs, multi-dimensional characters, a rich setting in an Ojibwe community, a compassionate and honest portrayal of historical wrongs, but more importantly, an account of the personal triumphs in the face of t</span>
  <span id="freeText7780123189410689380" style="display:none">I am on an enjoyable journey to read all of Louise Erdrich's novels. Tracks is my third novel of hers and the first in the Love Medicine read-along in which many from bookstagram are participating (thanks Erin and Dani for organizing!). <br /><br />This book exemplifies Erdrich's writing: PERFECT paragraphs, multi-dimensional characters, a rich setting in an Ojibwe community, a compassionate and honest portrayal of historical wrongs, but more importantly, an account of the personal triumphs in the face of those obstacles. Yet, it is so different from the other two novels I have read by her (The Round House and Future Home of the Living God). She is at once versatile and consistent in her craft. Next up, Four Souls!</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('daxd65tpHcAACkkSAa/qBhnYXpW3SFE/a+5X48Tl0/eGebaNoUTa5YGZo/0+wXVa04C8pzZOzKt5UdAe4cd/hA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_23057477'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23057477-the-cherokee-rose?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Cherokee Rose<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/201655.Tiya_Miles?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Tiya Miles<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.63 avg rating &mdash; 273 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2015\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer1763722559083272849\">The Cherokee Rose, written by Tiya Miles, award-winning historian and recipient of a recent MacArthur \"Genius Grant,\" explores territory reminiscent of the works of Alice Walker, Octavia Butler, and Louise Erdrich. This luminous but highly accessible<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1763722559083272849\" style=\"display:none\">The Cherokee Rose, written by Tiya Miles, award-winning historian and recipient of a recent MacArthur &quot;Genius Grant,&quot; explores territory reminiscent of the works of Alice Walker, Octavia Butler, and Louise Erdrich. This luminous but highly accessible work examines a little-known aspect of America\'s past slaveholding by Southern Creeks and Cherokees and its legacy in the lives of three young women who are drawn to the Georgia plantation where scenes of extreme cruelty and equally extraordinary compassion once played out. The novel is based on historical sources about the Chief Vann House Historic Site in Chatsworth, Georgia, and the Moravian mission sponsored there in the early 1800s. Miles uncovered this fascinating history while researching her book The House on Diamond Hill. In The Cherokee Rose, she has fictionalized the story and introduced contemporary aspects to make this history more accessible.\n\nThe characters in The Cherokee Rose include Jinx, the free-spirited historian exploring her tribe\'s complicated racial history; Ruth, whose mother sought refuge from a troubled marriage in her beloved garden and the cosmetic empire she built from its bounty; Cheyenne, the Southern black debutante seeking to connect with a meaningful personal history; and, hovering above them all, the spirit of long-gone Mary Ann Battis, a young woman suspected of burning a mission to the ground and then disappearing from tribal records. The story of the women\'s discoveries about the secrets of a Cherokee plantation traces their attempts to connect with the strong spirits of the past and reconcile the conflicts in their own lives.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1763722559083272849\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer Although Archilde plans to leave the reservation after a brief visit, his entanglements delay his departure until he faces destruction by the white man\'s law.\n\nIn an early review of The Surrounded, P<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6175451061368549924\" style=\"display:none\">The massacre of Cheyenne and Arapaho women and children by U.S. soldiers at Sand Creek in 1864 was a shameful episode in American history, and its battlefield was proposed as a National Historic Site in 1998 to pay homage to those innocent victims. Poet Simon Ortiz had honored those people seventeen years earlier in his own way. That book, from Sand Creek, is now back in print. Originally published in a small-press edition, from Sand Creek makes a large statement about injustices done to Native peoples in the name of Manifest Destiny. It also makes poignant reference to the spread of that ambition in other parts of the world—notably in Vietnam—as Ortiz asks himself what it is to be an American, a U.S. citizen, and an Indian. Indian people have often felt they have had no part in history, Ortiz observes, and through his work he shows how they can come to terms with this feeling. He invites Indian people to examine the process they have experienced as victims, subjects, and expendable resources—and asks people of European heritage to consider the motives that drive their own history and create their own form of victimization. Through the pages of this sobering work, Ortiz offers a new perspective on history and on America. Perhaps more important, he offers a breath of hope that our peoples might learn from each other:This America has been a burden of steel and mad death, but, look now, there are flowers and new grass In her forties Harness decided to get serious about finding answers when, conducting oral histories, she talked with other transracial adoptees. In her fifties she realized that the concept of “home” she had attributed to the reservation existed only in her imagination. Making sense of her family, the American Indian history of assimilation, and the very real—but culturally constructed—concept of race helped Harness answer the often puzzling questions of stereotypes, a sense of nonbelonging, the meaning of family, and the importance of forgiveness and self-acceptance. In the process Bitterroot also provides a deep and rich context in which to experience The Crown of Columbus chronicles the adventures of a pair of mismatched lovers--Vivi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7493199466582192086\" style=\"display:none\">In their only fully collaborative literary work, Michael Dorris and Louise Erdrich have written a gripping novel of history, suspense, recovery, and new beginnings.  The Crown of Columbus chronicles the adventures of a pair of mismatched lovers--Vivian Twostar, a divorced, pregnant anthropologist, and Roger Williams, a consummate academic, epic poet, and bewildered father of Vivian\'s baby--on their quest for the truth about Christopher Columbus and themselves.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/9388.Louise_Erdrich">About Louise Erdrich</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/9388.Louise_Erdrich"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1462224430p3/9388.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15538080460147444120">Karen Louise Erdrich is a American author of novels, poetry, and children's books. Her father is German American and mother is half Ojibwe and half French American. She is an enrolled member of the Anishinaabe nation (also known as Chippewa). She is widely acclaimed as one of the most significant Native writers of the second wave of what critic Kenneth Lincoln has called the Native American Renais</span>
  <span id="freeText15538080460147444120" style="display:none">Karen Louise Erdrich is a American author of novels, poetry, and children's books. Her father is German American and mother is half Ojibwe and half French American. She is an enrolled member of the Anishinaabe nation (also known as Chippewa). She is widely acclaimed as one of the most significant Native writers of the second wave of what critic Kenneth Lincoln has called the Native American Renaissance.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.answers.com/topic/louise-erdrich">http://www.answers.com/topic/louise-e...</a><br /><br />From a book description:<br /><p>Author Biography: </p><p>Louise Erdrich is one of the most gifted, prolific, and challenging of contemporary Native American novelists. Born in 1954 in Little Falls, Minnesota, she grew up mostly in Wahpeton, North Dakota, where her parents taught at Bureau of Indian Affairs schools. Her fiction reflects aspects of her mixed heritage: German through her father, and French and Ojibwa through her mother. She worked at various jobs, such as hoeing sugar beets, farm work, waitressing, short order cooking, lifeguarding, and construction work, before becoming a writer. She attended the Johns Hopkins creative writing program and received fellowships at the McDowell Colony and the Yaddo Colony. After she was named writer-in-residence at Dartmouth, she married professor Michael Dorris and raised several children, some of them adopted. She and Michael became a picture-book husband-and-wife writing team, though they wrote only one truly collaborative novel, <i>The Crown of Columbus</i> (1991).</p><p><i>The Antelope Wife</i> was published in 1998, not long after her separation from Michael and his subsequent suicide. Some reviewers believed they saw in <i>The Antelope Wife</i> the anguish Erdrich must have felt as her marriage crumbled, but she has stated that she is unconscious of having mirrored any real-life events.</p><p>She is the author of four previous bestselling andaward-winning novels, including <i>Love Medicine; The Beet Queen; Tracks; and The Bingo Palace. </i>She also has written two collections of poetry, <i>Jacklight, and Baptism of Desire.</i> Her fiction has been honored by the National Book Critics Circle (1984) and <i>The Los Angeles Times</i> (1985), and has been translated into fourteen languages. </p><p>Several of her short stories have been selected for O. Henry awards and for inclusion in the annual <i>Best American Short Story</i> anthologies. <i>The Blue Jay's Dance,</i> a memoir of motherhood, was her first nonfiction work, and her children's book, <i>Grandmother's Pigeon,</i> has been published by Hyperion Press. She lives in Minnesota with her children, who help her run a small independent bookstore called The Birchbark.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="15538080460147444120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
